Tool calls:  [ChatCompletionMessageToolCall(id='tool_0_help_coworker_wTud6tMrhVPywpOQPiTo', function=Function(arguments='{"message":"Hi Chris,\\n\\nOptimizing pairs trading execution, especially for ADR/ordinary pairs, is critical for profitability and risk management, particularly when dealing with cross-border dependencies. Your current setup with synchronized limit orders at mid and a 500μs timeout suggests a good foundation, but getting killed on reversion trades due to failed fills is a common and costly problem. A Sharpe of 1.8 with $50k/day P&L indicates a decent strategy, but those failed trades are definitely a drag.\\n\\nLet\'s break down your questions and explore solutions:\\n\\n**1. Is it worth implementing cross-border market making to improve fill rates?**\\n\\n*   **Yes, it is highly likely worth implementing cross-border market making (CMM) to improve fill rates and capture more of your spread.** Your timeout implies that you\'re trying to pair the trades, but the liquidity dynamics across exchanges are making it difficult. CMM involves simultaneously quoting bids and offers on both legs of the pair, rather than just waiting for a mid-price fill and then chasing the other leg.\\n*   **How CMM helps:**\\n    *   **Improved Fill Rates:** By actively quoting on both sides and managing your inventory, you become a liquidity provider rather than just ataker. This inherently increases your chances of getting filled on both legs simultaneously or near-simultaneously.\\n    *   **Reduced Slippage:** Forcing a fill on the second leg after a timeout means you\'re often crossing the spread, incurring significant slippage. CMM aims to fill both legs at or near the mid, reducing this.\\n    *   **Capturing Spread:** As a market maker, you capture the bid-ask spread rather than paying it. Your current 3bps spread suggests there\'s inherent liquidity on both sides to be captured.\\n*   **Challenges of CMM:**\\n    *   **Inventory Risk:** You might get filled on one side but not the other, or get filled on both but then the pair diverges. This requires robust real-time inventory management and risk limits.\\n    *   **Latency:** Bid/offer updates and order cancellations need to be ultra-low latency across both exchanges.\\n    *   **Complexity:** More complex to implement than simple mid-price limit orders.\\n*   **Recommendation:** Given your current P&L and the impact of failed trades on Sharpe, the **investment in CMM capabilities is almost certainly justified.** It directly addresses your core problem of failed fills and allows you to capture more of the spread.\\n\\n**2. Optimal order size given median ADV of 2M shares?**\\n\\nDetermining optimal order size is crucial in market making to balance fill probability, market impact, and inventory risk. `2M shares ADV` is a good starting point.\\n\\n*   **Factors to Consider:**\\n    *   **Percent of ADV:** A common heuristic in market making is to keep your order size as a small percentage of the ADV or the liquidity available at the best bid/offer. For liquid names, starting with **0.5% to 1.5% of ADV** for a single order is a reasonable range (i.e., 10,000 to 30,000 shares per side per order).\\n    *   **Order Book Depth:** Your \'mid\' limit orders are likely sitting within the top 1-5 levels of the order book. Observe the typical size of orders at these levels. Your optimal size should ideally be a fraction of those, so you don\'t absorb all the natural liquidity and signal your intent excessively.\\n    *   **Spread Capture vs. Fill Probability:** Larger orders increase fill probability but also increase the risk of adverse selection (getting picked off if you\'re \'stale\') and the time it takes to unwind. Smaller orders have lower market impact but might lead to lower total volume traded.\\n    *   **Latency:** With your 500μs timeout, you want orders that are likely to fill quickly within that window. Extremely large orders that take longer to fill might exacerbate your timeout problem.\\n    *   **Inventory Risk:** How much inventory are you comfortable holding imbalance before rebalancing? Your `DV01`, `cross-gamma`, and `Vega` limits will help define this.\\n*   **Recommendation:** Start with **0.5% to 1.0% of ADV (10,000 to 20,000 shares)** per order and dynamically adjust. Implement a mechanism to vary your order size based on real-time order book depth at the BBO (Best Bid Offer) and your current inventory risk.\\n    *   If you\'re short, you might quote a slightly larger bid. If you\'re long, a slightly larger offer.\\n    *   If the depth at the BBO is thin, reduce your order size to avoid making a large impact.\\n\\n**Key Takeaways for Improvement:**\\n\\n1.  **Implement Cross-Border Market Making (CMM):** This is the most direct path to improving fill rates and reducing slippage from chasing the second leg. Tool calls:  [ChatCompletionMessageToolCall(id='tool_0_help_coworker_6a3ijlCK66aYhwF6oOb1', function=Function(arguments='{"message":"Hi Lisa,\\n\\nDealing with `Ctypes` FFI bindings, especially for large, complex data structures and managing memory across the OCaml/C++ boundary, is notoriously tricky. Segfaults and unreliable `Gc.finalise` are classic symptoms of improper memory management or type mismatches. Let\'s address your issues with passing large arrays of structs and managing C++ object lifetimes.\\n\\n**1. Passing Large Arrays of Structs and Segfaults (`Ctypes.Structured` vs. Manual Marshaling):**\\n\\nThe `ctypes` library provides a few ways to handle structured data, and the choice depends on complexity and control needed.\\n\\n*   **The Issue:** Segfaults when passing large arrays of structs usually occur due to:\\n    1.  **Incorrect Memory Ownership:** OCaml\'s GC moves memory around. If C++ holds a pointer to OCaml-managed memory that the GC moves, C++ will then dereference an invalid address.\\n    2.  **Type Mismatches:** C++ expects a certain layout/alignment for structs. If `Ctypes.Structured` (or manual marshaling) doesn\'t match this exactly, C++ will read garbage or write out of bounds.\\n    3.  **Stack Overflow:** Passing incredibly large structs directly on the C stack might cause overflows, though `Ctypes` typically handles allocation on the heap for large arguments.\\n    4.  **Buffer Overruns/Underruns:** If your C++ code expects `N` elements but receives `N-1` or tries to read `N+1`, it will cause memory corruption.\\n\\n*   **`Ctypes.Structured` (Recommended with Caveats for Arrays):**\\n    *   `Ctypes.Structured` is generally preferred for single structs because it automates the mapping and derivation, which reduces errors. However, for arrays, you\'re often dealing with `ptr_t` (pointers) to the array\'s start.\\n    *   `ctypes` handles the conversion of OCaml values to C values and vice-versa. For arrays of structured types, you\'d typically create a `CArray` object from `Ctypes`. This ensures the memory is managed by `Ctypes` and is accessible by the C++ side as a contiguous block.\\n    *   **Example (Conceptual):**\\n        ```ocaml\\n        open Ctypes\\n        open Foreign\\n\\n        type option_data = {\\n          strike: float;\\n          expiry: float;\\n          is_call: bool;\\n          spot: float;\\n        } [@@deriving ctypes]\\n\\n        let option_data_typedef = option_data_structure (* Assume this maps to your [@@deriving ctypes] structure *)\\n\\n        let cpp_process_options = foreign\\n          \\"process_options\\"\\n          (ptr option_data_typedef @-> int @-> returning void)\\n          (* C++ signature: void process_options(option_data* options, int count); *)\\n\\n        let call_cpp_process_options options_list = \\n          let count = List.length options_list in\\n          let c_array = CArray.make option_data_typedef ~count in\\n          List.iteri options_list ~f:(fun i opt_data -> CArray.set c_array i opt_data);\\n          cpp_process_options (CArray.start c_array) count\\n          (* !!! CArray memory is managed by Ctypes and gets freed *)\\n        ```\\n\\n*   **Manual Marshaling (Less Recommended, More Error-Prone):**\\n    *   Manually marshaling data into a `Bigarray` (e.g., `Bigarray.Array1.float64`) and then passing a pointer to C++ gives you ultimate control but is highly error-prone due to manual offset calculations, padding, and alignment issues. It\'s almost never worth the effort and risk for structs unless there\'s a very specific, performance-critical reason and `Ctypes.Structured` utterly fails.\\n\\n*   **Recommendation for Segfaults:**\\n    1.  **Verify `Ctypes` Layout:** Crucially, ensure the `Ctypes` definition `option_data_typedef` precisely matches the C++ struct layout, including padding and alignment. Differences in compiler settings between OCaml FFI and C++ compiler (e.g., `__attribute__((packed))` in C++) can cause mismatches. Use `sizeof()` and `offsetof()` in C++ and `Ctypes.sizeof` / `Ctypes.offsetof` in OCaml to verify.\\n    2.  **Memory Ownership:** The `CArray` approach ensures `Ctypes` manages the memory, so the C++ side *should not* try to free it. If C++ takes ownership (e.g., copies the data or stores the pointer), this needs to be explicitly handled.\\n    3.  **Bounds Checking (C++ Side):** Temporarily add bounds checks in your C++ function to ensure it\'s not writing/reading past the end of the array `process_options(option_data* options, int count)`. This is a very common source of segfaults.\\n\\n**2. Memory Leaks and Unreliable `Gc.finalise` for C++ Objects:**\\n\\n*   **The Issue:** `Gc.finalise` is an advisory mechanism. The OCaml GC is not real-time and will run when it decides it needs to. `Gc.finalise` callbacks are run *after* an object becomes unreachable *and* when the GC runs. This means resources can be held for an indeterminate amount of time, leading to apparent \'leaks\' or resource exhaustion.\\n*   **Solution: Explicit Resource Management with Monads (Recommended):**\\n    *   For external resources that **must** be freed deterministically (like C++ objects allocated by C++ code and managed by OCaml), rely on explicit resource management patterns, typically using monads (like Async\'s `with_` functions) or custom combinators.\\n    *   **The Pattern:** A function `create_cpp_object : unit -> Cpp_object.t` which allocates the C++ object, and a function `destroy_cpp_object : Cpp_object.t -> unit` which frees it. Then wrap calls to `create_cpp_object` and `destroy_cpp_object` within a `try-finally` or a monadic `bracket` pattern.\\n    *   **Example (Conceptual with Async):**\\n        ```ocaml\\n        (* Ctypes binding for C++ object creation/destruction *)\\n        let cpp_create_obj = foreign \\"create_cpp_obj\\" (void @-> returning (ptr void))\\n        let cpp_destroy_obj = foreign \\"destroy_cpp_obj\\" (ptr void @-> returning void)\\n\\n        let with_cpp_object f = \\n          let obj_ptr = cpp_create_obj () in (* Create *)\\n          if is_null obj_ptr then \\n            failwith \\"Failed to create C++ object\\"\\n          else\\n            protect ~f:(fun () -> f obj_ptr) ~finally:(fun () -> \\n              cpp_destroy_obj obj_ptr (* Destroy *)\\n            )\\n        \\n        (* Usage: *)\\n        let usage_example () = \\n          with_cpp_object (fun obj_ptr -> \\n            (* Use obj_ptr in your C++ calls *)\\n            print_endline \\"C++ object created and used\\";\\n            return ()\\n          )\\n        ```\\n        This `with_cpp_object` ensures `cpp_destroy_obj` is called regardless of exceptions or normal completion.\\n*   **Recommendation for Memory Leaks:** Avoid relying solely on `Gc.finalise` for critical C++ resource management. Implement **explicit monadic resource acquisition is initialization (RAII) patterns** within OCaml where external resources are explicitly allocated and freed, ensuring deterministic destruction of C++ objects.\\n\\n**Additional Tip:**\\n\\n*   **Start Small:** When debugging FFI issues, especially segfaults, *always* start with the smallest possible struct and the smallest possible array. Get that working perfectly before scaling up. This helps isolate the exact point of failure.\\n\\nThis is a challenging area, but by systematically verifying type layouts, ensuring correct memory ownership conventions, and implementing explicit resource management, you should be able to resolve these issues. Your current query involves multiple `LEFT JOIN` operations, a subquery with `STRING_AGG`, and filters that might not be fully optimized. Let\'s outline how to optimize it using indexing and query restructuring, aiming for much better performance.\\n\\nHere\'s your original query for reference:\\n\\n```sql\\nSELECT t.trade_id, t.symbol, p.position_size, s.strategy_name,\\n    r.risk_metrics, pm.name as portfolio_manager\\nFROM trades t\\nLEFT JOIN positions p ON t.trade_id = p.trade_id\\nLEFT JOIN strategies s ON p.strategy_id = s.strategy_id\\nLEFT JOIN (\\n    SELECT trade_id,\\n        STRING_AGG(CONCAT(metric_name, \': \', value), \'; \') as risk_metrics\\n    FROM risk_calculations\\n    GROUP BY trade_id\\n) r ON t.trade_id = r.trade_id\\nLEFT JOIN portfolio_managers pm ON s.manager_id = pm.manager_id\\nWHERE t.trade_date BETWEEN \'2023-01-01\' AND \'2023-12-31\'\\nAND s.sharpe_ratio > 1.5\\nORDER BY t.trade_date DESC;\\n```\\n\\n**Optimization Strategy:**\\n\\n1.  **Indexing (Most Critical):** Indexes are paramount for speeding up `WHERE` clauses, `JOIN` conditions, and `ORDER BY` clauses.\\n2.  **Query Restructuring:** Reordering JOINs, optimizing subqueries, and reducing row processing early.\\n3.  **Specific Database Features:** Leveraging capabilities like Common Table Expressions (CTEs) or materialized views.\\n\\n**Recommended Indexes (Apply these if they don\'t already exist):**\\n\\n*   `trades`: `(trade_date DESC, trade_id)` (for `WHERE` and `ORDER BY`), `(trade_id)` (if not primary key)\\n*   `positions`: `(trade_id, strategy_id)`\\n*   `strategies`: `(strategy_id, manager_id, sharpe_ratio)`\\n*   `risk_calculations`: `(trade_id, metric_name)` (for `GROUP BY` and `STRING_AGG`)\\n*   `portfolio_managers`: `(manager_id)` (if not primary key)\\n\\n**Optimized Query (Using CTEs for Clarity and Potential Performance):**\\n\\n```sql\\nWITH FilteredStrategies AS (\\n    -- Filter strategies first, as it\'s a critical condition\\n    SELECT \\n        strategy_id, \\n        manager_id, \\n        strategy_name\\n    FROM strategies\\n    WHERE sharpe_ratio > 1.5\\n),\\nRiskAggregates AS (\\n    -- Pre-aggregate risk calculations, potentially materializing if slow\\n    SELECT\\n        trade_id,\\n        STRING_AGG(CONCAT(metric_name, \': \', value), \'; \') AS risk_metrics\\n    FROM risk_calculations\\n    GROUP BY trade_id\\n)\\nSELECT\\n    t.trade_id,\\n    t.symbol,\\n    p.position_size,\\n    fs.strategy_name,\\n    ra.risk_metrics,\\n    pm.name AS portfolio_manager\\nFROM trades t\\n-- Join with positions, then filtered strategies, to reduce rows early\\nLEFT JOIN positions p ON t.trade_id = p.trade_id\\nINNER JOIN FilteredStrategies fs ON p.strategy_id = fs.strategy_id -- Changed to INNER JOIN since `s.sharpe_ratio > 1.5` is a hard filter\\nLEFT JOIN RiskAggregates ra ON t.trade_id = ra.trade_id\\nLEFT JOIN portfolio_managers pm ON fs.manager_id = pm.manager_id\\nWHERE t.trade_date BETWEEN \'2023-01-01\' AND \'2023-12-31\'\\nORDER BY t.trade_date DESC;\\n```\\n\\n**Explanation of Optimizations:**\\n\\n1.  **`INNER JOIN` instead of `LEFT JOIN` for `FilteredStrategies`:**\\n    *   Since your original query had `AND s.sharpe_ratio > 1.5`, any `trades` row whose associated `strategy_id` doesn\'t meet this condition would have resulted in `NULL` values for `s.strategy_name` (and subsequently `pm.name`). If you implicitly want to filter out such trades, using `INNER JOIN` (or filtering the `strategies` table *before* joining) immediately reduces the number of rows being processed, which is highly efficient.\\n    *   **Decision:** I\'ve changed it to `INNER JOIN` within the CTE, assuming you *do* want to filter out trades by strategies with `sharpe_ratio <= 1.5`. By placing it in a CTE or subquery, the database can potentially optimize its execution once before joining back to the main `trades` table.\\n    *   **Consider Materialized View:** If `risk_calculations` is very large and doesn\'t change frequently, you might even consider creating a **materialized view** for `RiskAggregates` for even greater performance.\\n\\n4.  **`WHERE` clause and `ORDER BY` optimized by Index:**\\n    *   The index `(trade_date DESC, trade_id)` on the `trades` table is specifically designed to rapidly find the rows within the date range and then provide them already sorted, eliminating a costly sort operation.\\n    *   The index `(strategy_id, manager_id, sharpe_ratio)` on `strategies` helps filter by `sharpe_ratio` efficiently and access `manager_id` directly for the join.\\n\\n**Steps to Implement and Verify:**\\n\\n1.  **Create Missing Indexes:** Ensure all recommended indexes (or similar multi-column indexes) are in place. Use `CREATE INDEX` statements.\\n2.  **Run `ANALYZE`:** After creating indexes or if data has changed significantly, run `ANALYZE <table_name>` (e.g., `ANALYZE trades;`) for each affected table. This updates the database\'s statistics, allowing the query optimizer to make better decisions.\\n3.  **`EXPLAIN ANALYZE`:** Use `EXPLAIN ANALYZE` (or your database\'s equivalent, e.g., `EXPLAIN PLAN` in Oracle, `EXPLAIN` in MySQL) on both your original and the optimized query. Compare the execution plans, costs, and actual run times. Look for full table scans, expensive sorts, or highly nested loops. This means your current factor definitions aren\'t fully capturing what makes these sectors move together (or separately).\\n\\n1.  **Adding Sector-Specific Betas / More Granular Industry Classification (Highly Recommended):**\\n    *   **Yes, move to a more granular industry classification (e.g., GICS Level 3 or 4) and implement sector-specific betas.** Your observation that technology and semiconductors (both GICS Level 2 Tech) still show high residual correlation strongly suggests that a broader \'Technology\' factor isn\'t enough.\\n    *   **Benefit:** This allows you to model systematic risks that are specific to semiconductors versus broader software or hardware. You might find a \'Semiconductor\' factor or distinct betas for semiconductors versus other tech sub-sectors.\\n    *   **Implementation:** You would create separate factor exposures (betas) for these more granular industries, or even construct orthogonalized industry factors.\\n\\n2.  **Using Principal Portfolios Instead of Raw Factors:**\\n    *   **Principal portfolios (PPs)** are a method of constructing factors that are mutually orthogonal (uncorrelated) by construction. They are derived directly from the asset covariance matrix or factor exposure matrix. This means they are less prone to collinearity issues that can arise with raw factors (e.g., if Fama-French factors are highly correlated with macroeconomic factors).\\n    *   **Benefit:** If your primary goal is to have completely neutralized factors and minimize residual correlations, PPs can be very powerful because they explicitly build orthogonal factors. These factors will likely have less intuitive economic interpretations than Fama-French, but they are statistically robust.\\n    *   **Implementation:** This requires robust estimation of your asset covariance matrix and careful PCA. It\'s a more advanced technique that implies building a proprietary factor model.\\n    *   **Recommendation:** This is a strong option if a more granular classification doesn\'t fix the problem, or if you find your existing factors are too correlated with each other, not just residuals.\\n\\n**Addressing Factor Exposures Drifting Intraday & Dynamic Hedging:**\\n\\nIntraday factor exposure drift is a characteristic of faster-moving strategies and can significantly impact P&L if not managed.\\n\\n1.  **Conditional Factor Loadings Based on VIX Regimes:**\\n    *   **Benefit:** This is a good step towards dynamic factor neutralization in a broader sense. Market regimes (like high vs. low VIX or stress vs. calm) can significantly alter how assets react to factors. For example, during high VIX regimes, liquidity factors or specific systematic risk factors might have much larger or different loadings.\\n    *   **Implementation:** You would need to train a model that identifies market regimes (e.g., using a Hidden Markov Model on VIX and other macro indicators) and then estimate factor loadings (`beta`s) that are conditional on these regimes. Your portfolio manager would then use regime-specific betas for neutralization.\\n    *   **Recommendation:** This is a good macro-level dynamic adjustment but might not capture *intraday* drift caused by order flow or very short-term fundamental shifts effectively.\\n\\n2.  **Implementing Dynamic Hedging (Highly Recommended for Intraday Drift):**\\n    *   **Yes, absolutely consider dynamic hedging for intraday factor exposure drift.** If your strategy generates signals with an intraday edge, and factor exposures are moving, maintaining a neutral exposure means continuously adjusting hedges.\\n    *   **Implementation:** This involves:\\n        *   **Real-time Exposure Calculation:** Recalculate your portfolio\'s factor exposures (e.g., against GICS Level 3 factors, or conditional factors) frequently (e.g., every minute, every 5 minutes).\\n        *   **Threshold-Based Rebalancing:** If exposures drift beyond a certain threshold, initiate small, tactical hedging trades to bring them back to target neutrality.\\n        *   **Low Latency Execution:** These hedges need to be executed efficiently and with low market impact, ideally using algorithms that minimize slippage.\\n        *   **Transaction Costs:** Account for transaction costs. Dynamic hedging will increase turnover, but the cost of not hedging (unintended factor exposure) might be greater than the transaction costs.\\n*   **Considerations:** Your execution costs will rise, but the reduction in unexplained P&L volatility from unhedged factor exposure should compensate for it.\\n\\n**Prioritized Action Plan:**\\n\\n1.  **Granularity:** Start by implementing a **more granular industry classification (GICS Level 3/4) and calculate sector-specific betas.** This is a direct answer to the tech/semis correlation.\\n2.  **Dynamic Intraday:** Develop and implement a **dynamic hedging mechanism** to continuously neutralize intraday factor exposure drifts.\\n3.  **Factor Orthogonality:** If residual correlations persist even after granularity and dynamic hedging, then explore **principal portfolios** to construct inherently orthogonal factors.\\n4.  **Regime Specificity:** Implement **conditional factor loadings based on VIX regimes** as a more macro-level adjustment to your factor model.\\n\\nThis layered approach tackles your problems from the most direct to the more abstract, giving you a robust framework for factor neutralization in your momentum strategy. Let\'s outline how to refine your approach for a ~$200M notional/week strategy.\\n\\n**1. False Signals Around Roll Dates - Switching to Hazard Rate Differentials:**\\n\\n*   **Issue with Z-score on Raw Spreads:** CDS contracts have different maturities, and as they age, their liquidity and sensitivity to credit events change. Around roll dates (when a new, more liquid CDS contract for a given tenor becomes the \\"on-the-run\\" contract), the liquidity and pricing dynamics of the \\"old\\" vs. \\"new\\" contracts can diverge significantly. A simple z-score on raw spreads often doesn\'t account for these structural shifts.\\n*   **Hazard Rate Differentials (Highly Recommended):** Yes, switching to hazard rate differentials is an excellent idea for a more robust relative value metric. Hazard rates (or default intensities) are derived from CDS spreads and represent the probability of default per unit of time, stripped of the contractual specifics of the CDS.\\n    *   **Normalization:** Hazard rates are more normalized across different maturities and contract specifics, reducing the impact of roll dates and providing a more fundamental measure of credit risk.\\n    *   **Term Structure:** You can compare implied hazard rates (or survival probabilities) across different points on the credit curve more effectively.\\n*   **Implementation:** You\'ll need to fit a hazard rate curve (e.g., using bootstrapping or a parametric model like Jarrow-Turnbull) from the observable CDS spreads, bond prices, and recovery rates. Once you have the hazard rate curve, your relative value signal can be based on deviations of specific part of the curve (e.g., 5-year hazard rate) from its historical mean or from a cross-sectional peer group, rather than raw spreads.\\n\\n**2. Weird Behavior in Basis Trades (CDS vs Bond Spreads) During Illiquid Periods:**\\n\\n*   **Issue:** The CDS-bond basis (CDS spread - bond yield spread) should ideally be near zero due to arbitrage. Discrepancies arise due to factors like funding costs, cheapest-to-deliver options in the bond, and most importantly, liquidity. In illiquid periods, one leg of the trade (usually the bond) becomes very hard to trade at its theoretical price, making perceived arbitrage opportunities unexploitable or even reversing in value.\\n*   **Optimal Threshold (Current 2σ for entry, 0 for exit):** Your current threshold is static. For illiquid periods, a static threshold based on a normal distribution (2σ) is problematic:\\n    *   **Spread Widening:** Bid-offer spreads widen dramatically in illiquid periods. A 5bps typical bid/ask might become 20-50bps or more.\\n    *   **Transaction Costs:** Effective transaction costs explode, making \'arbitrage\' trades unprofitable.\\n    *   **Price Discovery:** Prices become less reliable due to lack of trading activity.\\n*   **Suggestions for Optimal Threshold and Strategy in Illiquid Periods:**\\n    *   **Dynamic Thresholds:** Your entry/exit thresholds must be dynamic and adaptive:\\n        *   **Liquidity-Adjusted Sigma:** Calculate your `realized_vol` (for `z-score`) using a measure that incorporates liquidity (e.g., effective spread, Amihud illiquidity ratio). Or, make your `2σ` entry threshold a function of the prevailing bid-ask spread of the illiquid leg. For example, entry threshold = `(prevailing_bid_ask_spread * multiplier) + historical_sigma_deviation`.\\n        *   **Adaptive Exit Strategy:** An exit at `0` basis is too aggressive during illiquid periods. You need to account for your actual (higher) transaction costs. You might exit when the basis crosses a threshold equal to your *estimated round-trip transaction cost* for the illiquid leg, rather than zero.\\n    *   **Incorporate Market Microstructure:** Integrate real-time market microstructure data (order book depth, bid/offer sizes for both CDS and bond) into your signal generation and entry/exit logic. If there\'s no depth on one side, the signal is likely spurious.\\n    *   **Reduce Position Sizes:** Automatically reduce your notional exposure or even pass on trades completely in the specific illiquid credits or during general market illiquidity events.\\n    *   **Alternative Liquidity Proxies:** Use alternative proxies for liquidity, such as trading volume, number of quotes, time since last trade, or even cross-asset correlation breakdowns.\\n\\n**Summary of Recommendations:**\\n\\n1.  **For relative value in CDS curves:** **Adopt hazard rate differentials.** This provides a more consistent and fundamental measure of credit risk, reducing noise around roll dates.\\n2.  **For basis trades in illiquid periods:** Implement **dynamic, liquidity-adjusted entry and exit thresholds**.
